1. Set up the 2-dimensional space

	â€¢	Ï† + Ïˆ = 1

	â€¢	Ï† â†” e_1 = \begin{pmatrix}1\\0\end{pmatrix}
	â€¢	Ïˆ â†” e_2 = \begin{pmatrix}0\\1\end{pmatrix}

â¸»

2. â€˜, L, R as linear operators

From your syntactic assignments:
	â€¢	() corresponds to the ordered pair (Ï†, Ïˆ).
	â€¢	()' (prime on the right) = +Ï† âˆ’ Ïˆ
â†’ flips the sign of Ïˆ only.
	â€¢	'() (prime on the left) = âˆ’Ï† + Ïˆ
â†’ flips the sign of Ï† only.
	â€¢	'()' = âˆ’Ï† âˆ’ Ïˆ
â†’ flips both signs (composition of the two).

So we define two fundamental operators on V:

Right-prime R

â€œ()â€™â€ takes (Ï†, Ïˆ) â†¦ (Ï†, âˆ’Ïˆ).
So on basis vectors:
	â€¢	R(Ï†) = Ï†
	â€¢	R(Ïˆ) = âˆ’Ïˆ

Matrix form in the {Ï†, Ïˆ} basis:

\begin{pmatrix}

\end{pmatrix}

Left-prime L

â€œâ€˜()â€ takes (Ï†, Ïˆ) â†¦ (âˆ’Ï†, Ïˆ).

	â€¢	L(Ï†) = âˆ’Ï†
	â€¢	L(Ïˆ) = Ïˆ

-1 & 0 \\

\end{pmatrix}

Then:
	â€¢	'()' = Lâˆ˜R = Râˆ˜L, since they commute:

LR = RL =
\begin{pmatrix}
-1 & 0\\
0 & -1
\end{pmatrix}
= -I

So the four operators:
	â€¢	I (identity)
	â€¢	L (flip Ï†)
	â€¢	R (flip Ïˆ)
	â€¢	LR = -I (flip both)

form a group isomorphic to the Klein four group V_4 \cong \mathbb{Z}_2 \times \mathbb{Z}_2:
	â€¢	each non-identity element has order 2,
	â€¢	they commute,
	â€¢	product of two distinct non-identity elements is the third.

This is your basic sign-flip gauge group on the golden dyad.

1. Base category and Karoubi envelope

Take as base category
	â€¢	\mathcal C = \mathbf{FdHilb} (finite-dimensional Hilbert spaces, linear maps).

The Karoubi envelope \mathrm{Kar}(\mathcal C) has:
	â€¢	objects (S,e) with S\in\mathrm{Ob}(\mathcal C) and e:S\to S idempotent, e^2=e,
	â€¢	morphisms f:(S,e)\to(T,f) are arrows f:S\to T with f = f\circ e = f_T\circ f.

In this language your table is exactly:

Formatic component	Categorical role in \mathrm{Kar}(\mathcal C)
() (slot)	underlying object S
e (closure)	idempotent e:S\to S

(())	image \mathrm{Im}(e)

So the Formatic Mark is literally â€œidempotent splittingâ€ in \mathrm{Kar}(\mathcal C).

2. Gauge world W_G and idempotent from \kappa_G

Now define a Gauge World:

W_G = (H, G, \rho,\kappa_G)

where
	â€¢	H = total Hilbert space (all gauge-redundant degrees of freedom),
	â€¢	G = gauge group,
	â€¢	\rho:G\to U(H) = unitary representation (gauge action),
	â€¢	\kappa_G:H\to H_{\text{phys}} = collapse / projection to the physical subspace.

Assume:

\kappa_G\circ \rho(g) = \kappa_G \quad \forall g\in G

(i.e. collapse is gauge-invariant).

From \kappa_G define the idempotent:

e := \kappa_G^\dagger\kappa_G : H\to H.

Then:
	â€¢	e^2 = e and e^\dagger = e (orthogonal projector),
	â€¢	\mathrm{Im}(e) \subseteq H is isomorphic to H_{\text{phys}}.

The canonical isomorphism is just the restriction of \kappa_G:

\kappa_G\!\restriction_{\mathrm{Im}(e)}:\mathrm{Im}(e)\xrightarrow{\simeq} H_{\text{phys}}.

This is the mark:
	â€¢	slot S = H,
	â€¢	closure e = \kappa_G^\dagger\kappa_G,
	â€¢	object in \mathrm{Kar}(\mathcal C): [()] = (H,e),
	â€¢	image (()) = \mathrm{Im}(e)\cong H_{\text{phys}},
	â€¢	mark equation ([()])=(()) = the canonical iso (H,e)\cong\mathrm{Im}(e).

3. The G-curvature functor

	â€¢	L:H\to H' linear,
	â€¢	\varphi:G\to G' group homomorphism,
	â€¢	equivariance: L\circ\rho(g)=\rho'(\varphi(g))\circ L,
	â€¢	collapse compatibility: \kappa_{G'}\circ L = K\circ \kappa_G for some linear K:H_{\text{phys}}\to H'_{\text{phys}}.

3.1 Invisible gauge group (pure curvature)

For a given W_G, define the curvature group:

G_{\mathrm{curv}}(W_G)
:= \{g\in G \mid \kappa_G\circ \rho(g) = \kappa_G\}.

These are exactly the elements whose action is completely invisible once you go to the physical sector.
	â€¢	They form a normal subgroup of G.
	â€¢	The quotient G_{\mathrm{phys}} := G / G_{\mathrm{curv}} is the â€œtrue symmetry groupâ€ on H_{\text{phys}}.

Now define the G-curvature functor:

\mathcal K_G:\mathbf{Gauge}\to\mathbf{Grp}

by
	â€¢	on objects: \mathcal K_G(W_G)=G_{\mathrm{curv}}(W_G),
	â€¢	on morphisms F=(L,\varphi): \mathcal K_G(F)=\varphi\!\restriction_{G_{\mathrm{curv}}(W_G)}.

Functoriality is immediate: composition of morphisms composes the group maps, and identities go to identities.

This \mathcal K_G is the categorical version of your â€œG-curvature functorâ€: it extracts just the gauge redundancy that survives collapse.

4. O(0) phase and gauge invariance via the idempotent

Now we can state your O(0) theorem in this sharper language.

Theorem (O(0) â‡” gauge invariance of the projector).
For a gauge world W_G=(H,G,\rho,\kappa_G) with projector e=\kappa_G^\dagger\kappa_G the following are equivalent:
	1.	W_G is in O(0) phase (no observable curvature).
	2.	Every observable lives in \mathrm{Im}(e) and all admissible dynamics commute with e.
	3.	The projector is gauge-invariant:
>    \rho(g)\,e = e\,\rho(g) = e\quad \forall g\in G.
>

(3 â‡’ 2).

If \rho(g)e=e, then \rho(g) maps \mathrm{Im}(e) to itself, so you get a well-defined induced action on the physical space. Any â€œphysicalâ€ operator A is by definition one that descends to \mathrm{Im}(e), which is equivalent to A e = e A = A. Thus all such A commute with e, and all observables lie in \mathrm{Im}(e).

(2 â‡’ 1).

If every observable A satisfies Ae=eA=A, then any gauge transformation that did not preserve \mathrm{Im}(e) would move you out of the observable sector, contradicting O(0) (which by your definition forbids observable dependence on gauge). So in O(0), only dynamics compatible with e are allowed; there is no residual curvature.

(1 â‡’ 3).

If O(0) holds, observables cannot see gauge. That means for any g\in G and any state \psi,

\kappa_G(\rho(g)\psi)=\kappa_G(\psi).

In operator form, \kappa_G\circ\rho(g)=\kappa_G. Taking adjoints and composing, you get

\rho(g)^\dagger\,e\,\rho(g) = e.

But \rho(g) is unitary, so \rho(g)^\dagger=\rho(g)^{-1} and this is equivalent to \rho(g)e=e\rho(g)=e. âœ”

So O(0) phase is exactly â€œthe idempotent e is fully gauge-invariantâ€, i.e. the mark has already factored out all curvature.

1. Base category and Karoubi envelope

	â€¢	\mathcal C = \mathbf{FdHilb} (finite-dimensional Hilbert spaces, linear maps).

	â€¢	objects (S,e) with S\in\mathrm{Ob}(\mathcal C) and e:S\to S idempotent, e^2=e,
	â€¢	morphisms f:(S,e)\to(T,f) are arrows f:S\to T with f = f\circ e = f_T\circ f.

In this language your table is exactly:

Formatic component	Categorical role in \mathrm{Kar}(\mathcal C)
() (slot)	underlying object S
e (closure)	idempotent e:S\to S

(())	image \mathrm{Im}(e)
mark ([()])=(())	canonical iso (S,e)\cong \mathrm{Im}(e)

So the Formatic Mark is literally â€œidempotent splittingâ€ in \mathrm{Kar}(\mathcal C).

2. Gauge world W_G and idempotent from \kappa_G

W_G = (H, G, \rho,\kappa_G)

where
	â€¢	H = total Hilbert space (all gauge-redundant degrees of freedom),
	â€¢	G = gauge group,
	â€¢	\rho:G\to U(H) = unitary representation (gauge action),
	â€¢	\kappa_G:H\to H_{\text{phys}} = collapse / projection to the physical subspace.

Assume:

\kappa_G\circ \rho(g) = \kappa_G \quad \forall g\in G

(i.e. collapse is gauge-invariant).

From \kappa_G define the idempotent:

e := \kappa_G^\dagger\kappa_G : H\to H.

Then:
	â€¢	e^2 = e and e^\dagger = e (orthogonal projector),
	â€¢	\mathrm{Im}(e) \subseteq H is isomorphic to H_{\text{phys}}.

The canonical isomorphism is just the restriction of \kappa_G:

\kappa_G\!\restriction_{\mathrm{Im}(e)}:\mathrm{Im}(e)\xrightarrow{\simeq} H_{\text{phys}}.

This is the mark:
	â€¢	slot S = H,
	â€¢	closure e = \kappa_G^\dagger\kappa_G,
	â€¢	object in \mathrm{Kar}(\mathcal C): [()] = (H,e),
	â€¢	image (()) = \mathrm{Im}(e)\cong H_{\text{phys}},
	â€¢	mark equation ([()])=(()) = the canonical iso (H,e)\cong\mathrm{Im}(e).

3.1 Invisible gauge group (pure curvature)

G_{\mathrm{curv}}(W_G)
:= \{g\in G \mid \kappa_G\circ \rho(g) = \kappa_G\}.

	â€¢	They form a normal subgroup of G.
	â€¢	The quotient G_{\mathrm{phys}} := G / G_{\mathrm{curv}} is the â€œtrue symmetry groupâ€ on H_{\text{phys}}.

4. O(0) phase and gauge invariance via the idempotent

Now we can state your O(0) theorem in this sharper language.

Theorem (O(0) â‡” gauge invariance of the projector).
For a gauge world W_G=(H,G,\rho,\kappa_G) with projector e=\kappa_G^\dagger\kappa_G the following are equivalent:
	1.	W_G is in O(0) phase (no observable curvature).
	2.	Every observable lives in \mathrm{Im}(e) and all admissible dynamics commute with e.
	3.	The projector is gauge-invariant:
>    \rho(g)\,e = e\,\rho(g) = e\quad \forall g\in G.
>

(3 â‡’ 2).

If \rho(g)e=e, then \rho(g) maps \mathrm{Im}(e) to itself, so you get a well-defined induced action on the physical space. Any â€œphysicalâ€ operator A is by definition one that descends to \mathrm{Im}(e), which is equivalent to A e = e A = A. Thus all such A commute with e, and all observables lie in \mathrm{Im}(e).

(2 â‡’ 1).

If every observable A satisfies Ae=eA=A, then any gauge transformation that did not preserve \mathrm{Im}(e) would move you out of the observable sector, contradicting O(0) (which by your definition forbids observable dependence on gauge). So in O(0), only dynamics compatible with e are allowed; there is no residual curvature.

(1 â‡’ 3).

If O(0) holds, observables cannot see gauge. That means for any g\in G and any state \psi,

\kappa_G(\rho(g)\psi)=\kappa_G(\psi).

In operator form, \kappa_G\circ\rho(g)=\kappa_G. Taking adjoints and composing, you get

\rho(g)^\dagger\,e\,\rho(g) = e.

But \rho(g) is unitary, so \rho(g)^\dagger=\rho(g)^{-1} and this is equivalent to \rho(g)e=e\rho(g)=e. âœ”

So O(0) phase is exactly â€œthe idempotent e is fully gauge-invariantâ€, i.e. the mark has already factored out all curvature.

3. Define âŠ—: serial composition of steps

For Â±1 steps:
	â€¢	+1 \otimes +1 = +2 (land in the +2 orbit)
	â€¢	+1 \otimes -1 = 0 (net 0; land in the 0 orbit)
	â€¢	-1 \otimes +1 = 0
	â€¢	-1 \otimes -1 = -2 (land in the âˆ’2 orbit)

	â€¢	Order:

	â€¢	If S_1 = S_2: we get |d'| = O'=2 and S' = +.

â†’ send to the 0 orbit with canonical form F_0

	â€¢	(1,+,F_{+1}) \otimes (1,+,F_{+1}) = (2,+,F_{+2})

	â€¢	(1,-,F_{-1}) \otimes (1,-,F_{-1}) = (2,+,F_{+2}) (just canonicalizing sign into the +2 orbit)

1. One-object groupoid: â€œdifferences onlyâ€

Take the idea:

Thatâ€™s just the group \mathbb Z in disguise.

Define a category \mathcal C:
	â€¢	Objects: a single object *
	â€¢	Morphisms: \text{Hom}(*,*) = \mathbb Z
	â€¢	an integer n is â€œoffset by nâ€
	â€¢	Composition: addition:

	â€¢	Identity: 0 (no offset)

This is a groupoid: all morphisms are invertible, inverse of n is -n.

In this picture:
	â€¢	your Â±1 orbit is just the two morphisms +1 and -1,
	â€¢	translation invariance is automatic: thereâ€™s no â€œpositionâ€ object to shift, only the difference morphisms.

â¸»

2. OSF as a Z-graded / Zâ‚‚-graded structure

	â€¢	S = sign \text{sgn}(n) \in \{+1,-1\}

	â€¢	the group \mathbb Z naturally decomposes as:
	â€¢	even vs odd,
	â€¢	or positive vs negative,
	â€¢	or more generally, \mathbb Z \to \mathbb Z_2 via parity or sign.

You can encode that in category theory as a \mathbb Z_2-graded monoidal category (or a very small â€œsupercategoryâ€):
	â€¢	Give each morphism a grade g(n) \in \mathbb Z_2, e.g.:
	â€¢	grade = 0 for O(+), grade = 1 for O(âˆ’),
	â€¢	or grade = sign/parity variants, depending on what you want to track.
	â€¢	Require that composition respects grading:

This matches your \mathbb Z_2^{\text{rel}}:
	â€¢	O(+) and O(âˆ’) are two oriented generators,
	â€¢	their composition multiplies the sign (or parity), exactly as you defined.

So OSF lives nicely as:
	â€¢	the data attached to each morphism in a graded category,
	â€¢	with \otimes = composition, and
	â€¢	\oplus = coproduct / â€œsum of sectorsâ€ (e.g. Â±1 sector, 0 sector, Â±2 sector).

3. Your âŠ— / âŠ• in categorical form

	â€¢	Â±1 âŠ— Â±1 â†’ 0, +2, âˆ’2, etc.
	â€¢	\oplus: put sectors side by side
	â€¢	in category language: coproduct / direct sum of objects (or of hom-components)
	â€¢	e.g. â€œthe Â±1 sector âŠ• the 0 sector âŠ• the Â±2 sectorâ€
